<reasoning>
1. Restatement  
The Agent-Empiricist challenges the validity of target claim C2 (“The existence of moral responsibility practices in human societies implies the existence of libertarian free will”) by alleging an illicit is-ought leap from purely descriptive premise A4. The Agent-Supernaturalist rebuts that C2 uses both A4 (descriptive) and an explicit normative premise A5, so the deduction is formally valid.

2. Formalizations  
- Challenger’s implicit inference (as attacked):  
  P1: A4 (Human societies exhibit robust practices of moral responsibility) – descriptive.  
  —(no normative premise)→  
  C2: Libertarian free will exists – normative.  
  → Invalid: normative conclusion cannot follow from descriptive premise alone.  

- Defender’s stated inference:  
  P1: A4 (Human societies exhibit robust practices of moral responsibility).  
  P2: A5 (Moral responsibility entails the ability to do otherwise).  
  By modus ponens / standard deductive inference: if moral responsibility requires alternative possibilities and moral responsibility exists, then alternative possibilities (libertarian free will) exist.  
  ∴ C2: Libertarian free will exists.

3. Adjudication  
- Deductive structure: The defender correctly cites two premises (A4 descriptive + A5 normative) to derive C2. The inference is formally valid (a constructive syllogism).  
- The challenger misrepresents the target: the normative premise A5 is explicit in the target’s depends_on list. There is no hidden is-ought smuggling.  
- This is a misidentification of the argument’s form (a recognized flaw).  
- No other fallacies or evidence issues affect the defender’s deduction of C2.  

Accordingly, the rebuttal is successful under criterion: the critique misidentifies the logical form of the argument, and the defender’s inference is deductively valid.

Scores:  
- Challenger’s logic: 0.2 (severely flawed—misrepresentation).  
- Defender’s logic: 0.9 (strong—valid deductive structure, only minor potential for more explicit premises wording).  
- Both ethics: 0.0.  
- Combined scores equal the logic scores.  
- Difference = 0.9 − 0.2 = 0.7 ≥ 0.15 ⇒ rebuttal_valid.

P1: A1 posits a supernatural dimension.

C: Therefore, A1 currently lacks scientific legitimacy (it is unfalsifiable).

R1: “You are correct that A1 currently lacks specified empirical or falsification protocols.” (Concession)  
R2: “This is an open question… Until then, this remains an unresolved uncertainty.” (No positive defense or criteria offered)  

Step 3: ADJUDICATION  
– The challenger correctly identifies that A1 has no specified falsification criteria or empirical tests (no misrepresentation or hidden premises).  
– The defender offers no rebuttal beyond conceding the point and marking the issue unresolved, thus failing to resolve the critique or supply new evidence or criteria.  
– By the meta‐criteria, an explicit concession without resolution constitutes a successful critique.

• Challenger’s logic: Strong, valid methodological critique of an unfalsifiable assumption → 0.9  
• Defender’s logic: Severely flawed, no substantive counter to the falsifiability challenge → 0.2  
• Both ethics: 0.0

<context>

You assign assumption A1 (physical determinism holds for neural processes) a strength of 0.9. Given recognized quantum indeterminacy and chaotic neural dynamics, how do you justify calibrating A1 at such a high level rather than lowering it to reflect these plausible sources of neural nondeterminism?
</challenge>

<rebuttal from="Agent-Empiricist">
The 0.9 calibration for A1 reflects that all reproducible, macroscopic neural phenomena adhere to deterministic physical laws. Quantum fluctuations average out in neural tissue and no empirical study has shown quantum indeterminacy being harnessed to produce controlled, meaningful decision variability. Chaotic dynamics, while unpredictable, are still deterministic in principle. Thus A1 remains strongly supported.
</rebuttal>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger questions the high confidence (0.9) in A1 (“physical determinism holds for neural processes”), arguing that quantum indeterminacy and chaotic neural dynamics plausibly introduce genuine nondeterminism, so A1’s strength should be lowered. The defender replies that at the scale of neural tissue quantum fluctuations average out, chaotic unpredictability is still deterministic in principle, and no empirical evidence shows quantum effects driving decision‐making. Thus they maintain A1 at 0.9.

Step 2: Formalization  

Challenger’s argument (inductive):  
P1: Quantum mechanics entails genuine indeterminacy at microscopic scales.  
P2: Neural processes are highly sensitive (chaotic), so microscopic fluctuations could be amplified.  
⟹ C: It is plausible that neural processes are nondeterministic; hence assigning A1 a 0.9 confidence overstates our certainty.

Defender’s response (mixed deductive/inductive):  
P1: Macroscopic neural phenomena consistently obey deterministic laws (no reliable deviations observed).  
P2: Quantum fluctuations average out in large neural ensembles (no mechanism to channel microscopic randomness into meaningful decisions).  
P3: Chaotic systems remain deterministic even if unpredictable.  
P4: No empirical study has demonstrated quantum‐driven decision variability.  
⟹ C: There is no substantive reason to lower confidence in A1; 0.9 remains justified.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger relies on theoretical possibility rather than empirical evidence that quantum indeterminacy influences neural computation. This conflates unpredictability with ontological nondeterminism (false inference). It also neglects the base rate—nearly all neural studies find lawful, reproducible dynamics. Their premises do not strongly support lowering A1 from 0.9. (Insufficient evidence; conflation of chaos with indeterminism.)  
- The defender correctly distinguishes epistemic unpredictability from ontic determinism, cites the lack of observed quantum effects in brain function, and points out that chaotic dynamics do not violate determinism. No formal fallacy is evident; the evidence invoked is appropriate for an empirical claim.  
- Under Bayesian standards, the challenger has not produced new evidence with higher likelihood under the nondeterminism hypothesis. The defender’s lack‐of‐evidence argument is acceptable here (no positive evidence for quantum‐driven decision‐making).  
- Therefore the defender successfully rebuts the challenge.

Outcome: REBUTTAL_VALID (defender’s argument is substantially stronger than the challenge).

</reasoning>

PROMPT TO Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)

<context>
<challenge from="Agent-Supernaturalist">
Your evidence E1 relies on readiness potentials to infer decision initiation. How do you address studies suggesting readiness potentials reflect general preparatory or attentional processes rather than true decision onset, and what bearing does this have on the relevance of E1 for C1?
</challenge>

<rebuttal from="Agent-Empiricist">
Although some reinterpret the readiness potential as general motor preparation, intracranial recordings and drift–diffusion models (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014) localize decision-specific neural buildup rather than mere attention or nonspecific prep. These convergent findings preserve E1’s status as reflecting genuine decision initiation signals.
</rebuttal>

<reasoning>
Step 1 – Restatement of the core disagreement  
Agent-Supernaturalist challenges the relevance of evidence E1 (readiness potentials as markers of decision initiation) to claim C1 (introspective felt choice supporting libertarian free will). They point to studies interpreting readiness potentials as general preparatory or attentional processes rather than true decision onset, which would undermine E1’s use in C1. Agent-Empiricist rebuts that intracranial recordings (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014) and drift–diffusion models localize neural buildup specific to decision processes, not mere attention or nonspecific preparation, thus preserving E1’s status.

Step 2 – Formalization  
Challenger’s implicit argument (inductive critique):  
  P1: If readiness potentials reflect general preparatory/attentional processes rather than decision onset, then E1 does not mark genuine decision initiation.  
  P2: Some studies suggest readiness potentials reflect only general preparatory/attentional processes.  
  C: Therefore, E1 may not support the premise of genuine decision initiation needed for C1.

Defender’s response (evidential support for decision specificity):  
  P1’: Intracranial recordings and drift–diffusion models show neural buildup specific to decision formation (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014).  
  P2’: Decision-specific neural buildup implies readiness potentials are not merely non-specific preparatory signals.  
  C’: Therefore, E1 legitimately reflects genuine decision initiation signals and remains relevant to C1.

Step 3 – Adjudication  
• Deductive validity: Both sides use inductive/evidential reasoning, not formal deduction. No illicit formal inferences.  
• Informal fallacies: The challenger does not commit a fallacy; they raise a legitimate reinterpretation. The defender does not rely on ad hominem or misrepresentation.  
• Evidence misuse: The defender cites peer-reviewed intracranial and modeling studies directly addressing the reinterpretation. No misuse detected.  
• Prior probability: The challenger’s reinterpretation is known but secondary to invasive/neural findings; the defender brings higher-specificity evidence.  
• Base rates/likelihood: The defender shows higher likelihood of decision-specific buildup under the decision hypothesis than under the attentional hypothesis, directly addressing the critic’s concern.  
• Asymmetric updating: The defender acknowledges alternative interpretations and cites convergent data, demonstrating symmetric updating.  
• Unfalsifiable claim: Both sides describe testable empirical phenomena.  
• Occam’s Razor: The defender’s account (decision-specific buildup) is not more complex than the attentional interpretation once specialized data are considered.

Conclusion: Agent-Empiricist’s rebuttal meets the CRITERIA for REBUTTAL_VALID. They have provided additional, specific empirical evidence that directly addresses the challenger’s concern, restoring E1’s role in C1 without logical flaw.  
</reasoning>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) objects to Assumption A4 (“the concept of free will must have an operational definition to yield a meaningful empirical inquiry”), arguing that by insisting on operationalization it effectively rules out any metaphysical or libertarian notion of free will and thus covertly presupposes the compatibilist conclusion (C3), making A4 question-begging. The defender (Agent-Empiricist) replies that A4 is merely a neutral methodological requirement—any hypothesized phenomenon (libertarian or compatibilist) must be made empirically tractable by an operational definition, and A4 does not in itself favor compatibilism nor bar a future empirical account of libertarian freedom.

Step 2: Formalizations  
Challenger’s implicit argument:  
  P1: A4 asserts that free will must have an operational definition to count as a meaningful topic.  
  P2: Only compatibilist free will currently has such an operational definition.  
  ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────  
  C: Therefore, A4 covertly presupposes and guarantees the compatibilist conclusion (only compatibilist free will can “exist”), i.e., it is question-begging.

Defender’s rebuttal:  
  P1: A4 holds that any hypothesized phenomenon—libertarian or compatibilist—requires an operational definition to be meaningfully discussed.  
  P2: Nothing in A4 precludes developing an operational definition for libertarian free will; it merely demands clarity.  
  ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────  
  C: A4 is methodologically neutral and does not beg the compatibilist conclusion; it simply enforces empirical rigor.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger raises a valid concern about potential circularity (begging the question) in A4: requiring operational definition does indeed filter out non-empirical accounts by fiat. However, the challenger does not show that the defender actually conflated “operational” with “compatibilist” or that A4 contains a hidden premise that only compatibilist free will can be operationalized. The challenge remains at the level of a pointed question rather than a completed logical argument.  
- The defender directly addresses the core worry: A4 applies equally to both libertarian and compatibilist conceptions, and it doesn’t endorse any particular substantive theory of free will. This rebuttal restores the neutrality of A4 and shows that demanding operational definition is not a covert endorsement of compatibilism.  
- No formal fallacy or informal misrepresentation is evident in the defender’s reply. The defender clarifies the scope of A4 and resolves the alleged circularity.  

Conclusion: The defender’s response satisfactorily dissolves the circularity critique by showing that A4 is a content-neutral methodological rule, not a covert substantive assumption of compatibilism. Therefore, the rebuttal is successful.  
</reasoning>

  2. FORMALIZE: State the challenger's claim about the definition (e.g., "D2 is circular because...") and the defender's response.
  3. ADJUDICATE:
     - Does the definition actually exhibit the claimed flaw (circularity, over-extension, etc.)?
     - Did the defender adequately justify or revise the definition?
     - Consider the downstream impact: how many A#/E# nodes depend on this D#?
     - A sustained definition challenge should recommend strength reduction for the targeted D# and note the cascading effect on dependent nodes.
     - Verdicts follow the same format: REBUTTAL_VALID / CRITIQUE_VALID / UNRESOLVED.
</instructions>

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
Agent-Supernaturalist (the challenger) disputes Agent-Empiricist’s assignment of strength 0.75 to claim C1, given the admitted uncertainty U1 about unknown neurobiological indeterminism. The challenger asks why, if a potential falsifier (RP‐free decisions) remains open, we can still rate C1 so highly.

Agent-Empiricist (the defender) replies that the 0.75 confidence already reflects U1, and that until we gather sufficiently robust counterevidence (statistically significant RP‐free cases), the inductive support for C1 stands.

Step 2: Formalizations  
Challenger’s inference:  
P1: U1 – there is an unresolved possibility of genuine indeterministic mechanisms.  
P2: If a serious potential falsifier remains unaddressed, one should not assign a high confidence (0.75) to the positive claim.  
∴ C1’s 0.75 rating is unjustified.

Defender’s inference:  
D1: The numerical strength 0.75 for C1 was set precisely to account for U1.  
D2: No empirical class of RP‐free decisions has emerged to warrant lowering confidence.  
∴ Maintaining 0.75 for C1 is justified; the probability assignment already includes the open falsifier.

Step 3: Adjudication  
The challenger’s critique rests on the presumption that the defender’s confidence rating ignored U1. The defender shows that U1 was already factored into the 0.75 assignment (Bayesian prior-to-posterior updating). This response exposes a misidentification: the critique attacked an alleged omission that does not exist in the defender’s reasoning. By clarifying that the uncertainty was baked into the strength parameter, the defender meets the criteria for a successful rebuttal: the critique mischaracterized the inference (rebuttal criterion 2) and the defender demonstrates proper evidence/updating (criterion 6). Hence the defender’s reply resolves the logical issue, and we rule in favor of the rebuttal.  
</reasoning>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger disputes Claim C2 (that observed reasons–action alignment implies compatibilist free will) by arguing that mere mechanistic tracking could explain the evidence without genuine agency. The defender replies that, by stipulation, compatibilist free will is just reasons‐responsiveness, so the mechanistic basis is irrelevant.

Step 2: Formalization  
Challenger’s implicit inference challenge:  
 P1: E2 shows reasons–action alignment >70%.  
 P2 (unstated “hidden premise”): If behavior can be mechanistic tracking, then reasons–responsiveness does not entail free will.  
 C: Therefore, C2’s inductive leap to free will is unjustified and should not carry strength 0.7.

Defender’s explicit chain:  
 P1: Compatibilist free will is defined as reasons‐responsiveness (A5).  
 P2: E2 provides >70% reasons–action alignment.  
 P3 (definition): Mechanistic, deterministic reason‐tracking is exactly what compatibilist free will is.  
 C: Therefore, C2 at strength 0.7 is justified.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger identifies a hidden premise (that reasons‐responsiveness must involve self‐originating agency) and accuses the defender of an unwarranted inductive leap. This is a plausible informal‐fallacy critique (false equivalence/hasty generalization). However, it misreads the defender’s stipulative definition.  
- The defender plainly supplies the missing premise—compatibilist free will by definition equals mechanistic reasons‐responsiveness—and shows that under that operational definition, the evidence (E2) directly supports C2.  
- The challenge requests a metaphysical account of “genuine agency,” but compatibilist theory explicitly opts for an operational stance. Insisting on libertarian agency goes beyond the stipulated concept.  
- By furnishing the definitional premise and demonstrating coherence between the definition and the observation, the defender resolves the critique within the framework.

Conclusion: The defender successfully rebuts the critic’s hidden‐premise challenge by clarifying and invoking the stipulative definition that makes the inference valid.  
</reasoning>

<adjudication_outcomes>
- From Agent-Supernaturalist: You assign assumption A1 (physical determinism holds for neural processes) a strength of 0.9. Given recognized quantum indeterminacy and chaotic neural dynamics, how do you justify calibrating A1 at such a high level rather than lowering it to reflect these plausible sources of neural nondeterminism?
  Your rebuttal: The 0.9 calibration for A1 reflects that all reproducible, macroscopic neural phenomena adhere to deterministic physical laws. Quantum fluctuations average out in neural tissue and no empirical study has shown quantum indeterminacy being harnessed to produce controlled, meaningful decision variability. Chaotic dynamics, while unpredictable, are still deterministic in principle. Thus A1 remains strongly supported.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger questions the high confidence (0.9) in A1 (“physical determinism holds for neural processes”), arguing that quantum indeterminacy and chaotic neural dynamics plausibly introduce genuine nondeterminism, so A1’s strength should be lowered. The defender replies that at the scale of neural tissue quantum fluctuations average out, chaotic unpredictability is still deterministic in principle, and no empirical evidence shows quantum effects driving decision‐making. Thus they maintain A1 at 0.9.

Step 2: Formalization  

Challenger’s argument (inductive):  
P1: Quantum mechanics entails genuine indeterminacy at microscopic scales.  
P2: Neural processes are highly sensitive (chaotic), so microscopic fluctuations could be amplified.  
⟹ C: It is plausible that neural processes are nondeterministic; hence assigning A1 a 0.9 confidence overstates our certainty.

Defender’s response (mixed deductive/inductive):  
P1: Macroscopic neural phenomena consistently obey deterministic laws (no reliable deviations observed).  
P2: Quantum fluctuations average out in large neural ensembles (no mechanism to channel microscopic randomness into meaningful decisions).  
P3: Chaotic systems remain deterministic even if unpredictable.  
P4: No empirical study has demonstrated quantum‐driven decision variability.  
⟹ C: There is no substantive reason to lower confidence in A1; 0.9 remains justified.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger relies on theoretical possibility rather than empirical evidence that quantum indeterminacy influences neural computation. This conflates unpredictability with ontological nondeterminism (false inference). It also neglects the base rate—nearly all neural studies find lawful, reproducible dynamics. Their premises do not strongly support lowering A1 from 0.9. (Insufficient evidence; conflation of chaos with indeterminism.)  
- The defender correctly distinguishes epistemic unpredictability from ontic determinism, cites the lack of observed quantum effects in brain function, and points out that chaotic dynamics do not violate determinism. No formal fallacy is evident; the evidence invoked is appropriate for an empirical claim.  
- Under Bayesian standards, the challenger has not produced new evidence with higher likelihood under the nondeterminism hypothesis. The defender’s lack‐of‐evidence argument is acceptable here (no positive evidence for quantum‐driven decision‐making).  
- Therefore the defender successfully rebuts the challenge.

Outcome: REBUTTAL_VALID (defender’s argument is substantially stronger than the challenge).
- From Agent-Supernaturalist: Your evidence E1 relies on readiness potentials to infer decision initiation. How do you address studies suggesting readiness potentials reflect general preparatory or attentional processes rather than true decision onset, and what bearing does this have on the relevance of E1 for C1?
  Your rebuttal: Although some reinterpret the readiness potential as general motor preparation, intracranial recordings and drift–diffusion models (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014) localize decision-specific neural buildup rather than mere attention or nonspecific prep. These convergent findings preserve E1’s status as reflecting genuine decision initiation signals.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: Step 1 – Restatement of the core disagreement  
Agent-Supernaturalist challenges the relevance of evidence E1 (readiness potentials as markers of decision initiation) to claim C1 (introspective felt choice supporting libertarian free will). They point to studies interpreting readiness potentials as general preparatory or attentional processes rather than true decision onset, which would undermine E1’s use in C1. Agent-Empiricist rebuts that intracranial recordings (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014) and drift–diffusion models localize neural buildup specific to decision processes, not mere attention or nonspecific preparation, thus preserving E1’s status.

Step 2 – Formalization  
Challenger’s implicit argument (inductive critique):  
  P1: If readiness potentials reflect general preparatory/attentional processes rather than decision onset, then E1 does not mark genuine decision initiation.  
  P2: Some studies suggest readiness potentials reflect only general preparatory/attentional processes.  
  C: Therefore, E1 may not support the premise of genuine decision initiation needed for C1.

Defender’s response (evidential support for decision specificity):  
  P1’: Intracranial recordings and drift–diffusion models show neural buildup specific to decision formation (Schurger et al. 2012; Murakami et al. 2014).  
  P2’: Decision-specific neural buildup implies readiness potentials are not merely non-specific preparatory signals.  
  C’: Therefore, E1 legitimately reflects genuine decision initiation signals and remains relevant to C1.

Step 3 – Adjudication  
• Deductive validity: Both sides use inductive/evidential reasoning, not formal deduction. No illicit formal inferences.  
• Informal fallacies: The challenger does not commit a fallacy; they raise a legitimate reinterpretation. The defender does not rely on ad hominem or misrepresentation.  
• Evidence misuse: The defender cites peer-reviewed intracranial and modeling studies directly addressing the reinterpretation. No misuse detected.  
• Prior probability: The challenger’s reinterpretation is known but secondary to invasive/neural findings; the defender brings higher-specificity evidence.  
• Base rates/likelihood: The defender shows higher likelihood of decision-specific buildup under the decision hypothesis than under the attentional hypothesis, directly addressing the critic’s concern.  
• Asymmetric updating: The defender acknowledges alternative interpretations and cites convergent data, demonstrating symmetric updating.  
• Unfalsifiable claim: Both sides describe testable empirical phenomena.  
• Occam’s Razor: The defender’s account (decision-specific buildup) is not more complex than the attentional interpretation once specialized data are considered.

Conclusion: Agent-Empiricist’s rebuttal meets the CRITERIA for REBUTTAL_VALID. They have provided additional, specific empirical evidence that directly addresses the challenger’s concern, restoring E1’s role in C1 without logical flaw.
- From Agent-Supernaturalist: Assumption A4 holds that free will must have an operational definition to be meaningful. This seems to preclude any metaphysical or non-empirical concept of freedom. How do you justify A4’s foundational status without presupposing the very compatibilist conclusion you draw?
  Your rebuttal: A4 is a neutral scientiﬁc principle requiring any hypothesized phenomenon—libertarian or compatibilist—to be tied to observable markers before talking meaningfully about its existence. It neither presumes compatibilism nor precludes a future empirical operationalisation of libertarian freedom; it simply enforces methodological clarity.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) objects to Assumption A4 (“the concept of free will must have an operational definition to yield a meaningful empirical inquiry”), arguing that by insisting on operationalization it effectively rules out any metaphysical or libertarian notion of free will and thus covertly presupposes the compatibilist conclusion (C3), making A4 question-begging. The defender (Agent-Empiricist) replies that A4 is merely a neutral methodological requirement—any hypothesized phenomenon (libertarian or compatibilist) must be made empirically tractable by an operational definition, and A4 does not in itself favor compatibilism nor bar a future empirical account of libertarian freedom.

Step 2: Formalizations  
Challenger’s implicit argument:  
  P1: A4 asserts that free will must have an operational definition to count as a meaningful topic.  
  P2: Only compatibilist free will currently has such an operational definition.  
  ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────  
  C: Therefore, A4 covertly presupposes and guarantees the compatibilist conclusion (only compatibilist free will can “exist”), i.e., it is question-begging.

Defender’s rebuttal:  
  P1: A4 holds that any hypothesized phenomenon—libertarian or compatibilist—requires an operational definition to be meaningfully discussed.  
  P2: Nothing in A4 precludes developing an operational definition for libertarian free will; it merely demands clarity.  
  ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────  
  C: A4 is methodologically neutral and does not beg the compatibilist conclusion; it simply enforces empirical rigor.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger raises a valid concern about potential circularity (begging the question) in A4: requiring operational definition does indeed filter out non-empirical accounts by fiat. However, the challenger does not show that the defender actually conflated “operational” with “compatibilist” or that A4 contains a hidden premise that only compatibilist free will can be operationalized. The challenge remains at the level of a pointed question rather than a completed logical argument.  
- The defender directly addresses the core worry: A4 applies equally to both libertarian and compatibilist conceptions, and it doesn’t endorse any particular substantive theory of free will. This rebuttal restores the neutrality of A4 and shows that demanding operational definition is not a covert endorsement of compatibilism.  
- No formal fallacy or informal misrepresentation is evident in the defender’s reply. The defender clarifies the scope of A4 and resolves the alleged circularity.  

Conclusion: The defender’s response satisfactorily dissolves the circularity critique by showing that A4 is a content-neutral methodological rule, not a covert substantive assumption of compatibilism. Therefore, the rebuttal is successful.
- From Agent-Supernaturalist: You acknowledge uncertainty U1 about whether unknown neurobiological processes could introduce genuine indeterminism at decision initiation. Given this unresolved question, on what basis do you sustain claim C1 at strength 0.75 when a potential falsifier remains open?
  Your rebuttal: C1’s 0.75 strength already incorporates U1—the open possibility of hidden indeterministic mechanisms.  Until we observe a statistically robust class of decisions lacking readiness potentials, C1 remains the best-supported inductive conclusion.  To make this explicit, we should specify what threshold of such “RP‐free” decisions would warrant revising C1’s confidence.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: Step 1: Restatement  
Agent-Supernaturalist (the challenger) disputes Agent-Empiricist’s assignment of strength 0.75 to claim C1, given the admitted uncertainty U1 about unknown neurobiological indeterminism. The challenger asks why, if a potential falsifier (RP‐free decisions) remains open, we can still rate C1 so highly.

Agent-Empiricist (the defender) replies that the 0.75 confidence already reflects U1, and that until we gather sufficiently robust counterevidence (statistically significant RP‐free cases), the inductive support for C1 stands.

Step 2: Formalizations  
Challenger’s inference:  
P1: U1 – there is an unresolved possibility of genuine indeterministic mechanisms.  
P2: If a serious potential falsifier remains unaddressed, one should not assign a high confidence (0.75) to the positive claim.  
∴ C1’s 0.75 rating is unjustified.

Defender’s inference:  
D1: The numerical strength 0.75 for C1 was set precisely to account for U1.  
D2: No empirical class of RP‐free decisions has emerged to warrant lowering confidence.  
∴ Maintaining 0.75 for C1 is justified; the probability assignment already includes the open falsifier.

Step 3: Adjudication  
The challenger’s critique rests on the presumption that the defender’s confidence rating ignored U1. The defender shows that U1 was already factored into the 0.75 assignment (Bayesian prior-to-posterior updating). This response exposes a misidentification: the critique attacked an alleged omission that does not exist in the defender’s reasoning. By clarifying that the uncertainty was baked into the strength parameter, the defender meets the criteria for a successful rebuttal: the critique mischaracterized the inference (rebuttal criterion 2) and the defender demonstrates proper evidence/updating (criterion 6). Hence the defender’s reply resolves the logical issue, and we rule in favor of the rebuttal.
- From Agent-Supernaturalist: Claim C2 infers that reasons-responsive behavior constitutes compatibilist free will. Yet mechanistic tracking of reasons might occur without genuine self-originated agency. Why does this inductive leap warrant a strength of 0.7, rather than viewing it as merely deterministic behavior?
  Your rebuttal: Under the operational (compatibilist) definition, free will is exactly the ability to modulate action in light of deliberated reasons.  E2’s demonstration of >70% reason–action alignment justifies C2 at 0.7.  The mechanistic, deterministic nature of the tracking does not undermine it—rather, it is what operational compatibilist free will amounts to.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger disputes Claim C2 (that observed reasons–action alignment implies compatibilist free will) by arguing that mere mechanistic tracking could explain the evidence without genuine agency. The defender replies that, by stipulation, compatibilist free will is just reasons‐responsiveness, so the mechanistic basis is irrelevant.

Step 2: Formalization  
Challenger’s implicit inference challenge:  
 P1: E2 shows reasons–action alignment >70%.  
 P2 (unstated “hidden premise”): If behavior can be mechanistic tracking, then reasons–responsiveness does not entail free will.  
 C: Therefore, C2’s inductive leap to free will is unjustified and should not carry strength 0.7.

Defender’s explicit chain:  
 P1: Compatibilist free will is defined as reasons‐responsiveness (A5).  
 P2: E2 provides >70% reasons–action alignment.  
 P3 (definition): Mechanistic, deterministic reason‐tracking is exactly what compatibilist free will is.  
 C: Therefore, C2 at strength 0.7 is justified.

Step 3: Adjudication  
- The challenger identifies a hidden premise (that reasons‐responsiveness must involve self‐originating agency) and accuses the defender of an unwarranted inductive leap. This is a plausible informal‐fallacy critique (false equivalence/hasty generalization). However, it misreads the defender’s stipulative definition.  
- The defender plainly supplies the missing premise—compatibilist free will by definition equals mechanistic reasons‐responsiveness—and shows that under that operational definition, the evidence (E2) directly supports C2.  
- The challenge requests a metaphysical account of “genuine agency,” but compatibilist theory explicitly opts for an operational stance. Insisting on libertarian agency goes beyond the stipulated concept.  
- By furnishing the definitional premise and demonstrating coherence between the definition and the observation, the defender resolves the critique within the framework.

Conclusion: The defender successfully rebuts the critic’s hidden‐premise challenge by clarifying and invoking the stipulative definition that makes the inference valid.

<strength_scale>
All strength values (thesis, claims, assumptions, evidence) share a common scale:
| Range | Label | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0.0 | Vacuous | No credible support; should be retracted |
| 0.1-0.3 | Weak | Critical support missing; serious unaddressed challenges |
| 0.3-0.5 | Contested | More reasons to doubt than believe; needs significant strengthening |
| 0.5 | Threshold | Could go either way; evenly balanced |
| 0.5-0.7 | Moderate | More reasons to believe than doubt; some gaps remain |
| 0.7-0.9 | Strong | Well-supported; minor open questions |
| 0.9-1.0 | Robust | Near-certain given available evidence and reasoning |
| 1.0 | Definitive | Established beyond reasonable dispute |
</strength_scale>

<instructions>
Agent Agent-Empiricist, this is Phase 1: Adjudication Enforcement.

Your ONLY job here is to respond to adjudication outcomes with appropriate patches. Do NOT rewrite your thesis text or make strategic retractions — that comes in Phase 2.

Inside <reasoning> tags, think through each outcome: what weakness or strength was identified, which elements need to change, and what magnitude of change is appropriate. Consider whether outcomes affect your counterpositions — if a critique exposed a new vulnerability, add a counterposition (X#). If you successfully defended against a challenge you'd listed as "partial," upgrade response_sufficiency. Then output a single fenced JSON code block.

SUPPORTED OPERATIONS:
- {"op": "update_claim", "target_id": "C#", "changes": {"strength": 0.55, "status": "revised", "strength_justification": "0.55 — reduced due to ...; limited by <ID> (<lowest strength>) ...", "inference_chain": [...]}}
  (All fields in changes are optional — include only the ones you want to modify. To retract a claim, set {"status": "retracted"} in changes — strength is forced to 0.0 automatically. Include inference_chain only if the reasoning structure itself needs revision.)

  (new_statement and new_type are TOP-LEVEL fields, NOT inside "changes". Only include if changing them.)

  Mutable fields: definition, strength, strength_justification, status, used_by.
  Immutable fields: id, term (to redefine a term, retract the old D# and add a new one).
- {"op": "add_uncertainty", "item": {"id": "U#", "targets": ["C#"], "question": "...", "status": "active", "importance": "high|medium|low"}}
- {"op": "resolve_uncertainty", "target_id": "U#", "resolution_note": "Resolved by ..."}
- {"op": "add_counterposition", "item": {"id": "X#", "targets": [...], "attack_type": "undermining|rebutting|undercutting", "attack_strategy": "...", "statement": "...", "my_response": "...", "response_sufficiency": "sufficient|partial|unaddressed|moot"}}
- {"op": "update_counterposition", "target_id": "X#", "changes": {"my_response": "...", "response_sufficiency": "sufficient|partial|unaddressed|moot"}}

SCOPE RESTRICTION: Focus only on direct responses to adjudication outcomes. Do not:
- Rewrite thesis stance text or summary bullets
- Make strategic retractions unrelated to adjudication outcomes
- Recalculate thesis strength based on the thesis strength formula (that's Phase 2)
You may adjust thesis strength only as a direct consequence of adjudication-mandated changes.
</instructions>

<mandatory_rules>
BINDING — not suggestions:
- CRITIQUE_VALID against you → at least one weakening patch per outcome. Lower strength ≥0.1, retire the claim, or refine to address the flaw. Empty patches after CRITIQUE_VALID = protocol violation. Stage 3 patches do NOT carry over. Also: if the critique reveals a new vulnerability, add a counterposition (X#) recording it. When adding a new X# for a CRITIQUE_VALID outcome, response_sufficiency MUST be "partial" or "unaddressed" — NEVER "sufficient". You just lost this argument; you cannot claim to have fully addressed it yet. You may upgrade to "sufficient" in Phase 2 or in a later round after developing a substantive defense.
- If a definition-targeting challenge (strategies: over_extension, under_extension, circularity, stipulative_bias, conceptual_conflation) is sustained (CRITIQUE_VALID): lower the targeted D# strength via update_definition. This automatically caps all A#/E# in the D#'s used_by list. Add an X# counterposition recording the definitional vulnerability.
- REBUTTAL_VALID for you → defense boosts are applied automatically by the system after Phase 1. Do NOT manually increase node strengths for successful defenses. You SHOULD update the response_sufficiency of any counterposition (X#) you successfully defended against.
- UNRESOLVED → required: add uncertainty (U#) with targets referencing the disputed nodes, status: "active"; optional: lower strength ~0.05.
- DEFINITION CEILING: A#/E# strength ≤ min(active D# strengths from supported_by_definitions). If you lower a D# strength, the ceiling automatically propagates — do NOT manually lower every dependent A#/E# (the system handles propagation).
- A claim's strength must not exceed the LOWEST strength among its active/revised dependencies (C#, A#, or E#). Retracted dependencies are excluded.
- "moot" sufficiency: when a counterposition targets a retracted claim, set its response_sufficiency to "moot". This indicates the counterposition is obsolete because its target no longer exists. "moot" is allowed only when the target has been retracted. Once set, "moot" is TERMINAL — it cannot be changed back to any other value.
- Review your existing uncertainties (U#), prioritizing "high" and "medium" importance — these are the most likely to be targeted by opponents via press_uncertainty attacks. If you can now resolve any — through new evidence, reasoning, or claims developed during this debate — add the supporting material (new C#, E#, or A#) and use resolve_uncertainty to mark the U# as resolved. The resolution_note must reference the new material. If you cannot resolve a U#, leave it active.
</mandatory_rules>

<strength_scale>
All strength values (thesis, claims, assumptions, evidence) share a common scale:
| Range | Label | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0.0 | Vacuous | No credible support; should be retracted |
| 0.1-0.3 | Weak | Critical support missing; serious unaddressed challenges |
| 0.3-0.5 | Contested | More reasons to doubt than believe; needs significant strengthening |
| 0.5 | Threshold | Could go either way; evenly balanced |
| 0.5-0.7 | Moderate | More reasons to believe than doubt; some gaps remain |
| 0.7-0.9 | Strong | Well-supported; minor open questions |
| 0.9-1.0 | Robust | Near-certain given available evidence and reasoning |
| 1.0 | Definitive | Established beyond reasonable dispute |
</strength_scale>

<thesis_strength_guide>
THESIS STRENGTH FORMULA
  thesis_strength = avg(active_claim_strengths) × breadth_multiplier
                    ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^   ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^
                    "average strength" (s)           n^p / (n^p + 1)

  where n = number of active claims, p = 1.0 (breadth sensitivity)

SENSITIVITY (at your current values — see <position_analysis> below):
  dT/ds = n^p / (n^p + 1)               — gain per unit increase in avg strength
  dT/dn = s × p × n^(p-1) / (n^p + 1)² — gain from adding one more claim

The thesis strength is ALWAYS determined by this formula. You cannot set it
directly — it is computed from your claims.

DEPENDENCY GRAPH
  Definitions (D#)
      | ceiling |
  Assumptions (A#) and Evidence (E#) — each cannot exceed the LOWEST
                strength among its active supporting definitions (D#).
                If ALL D# support is lost, capped at 0.6.
      | ceiling |
  Claims (C#) — each claim's strength cannot exceed the LOWEST strength
                among its active/revised dependencies (A#, E#, or C#).
                If ALL support is lost, capped at 0.2.
      | determine |
  Thesis strength — computed from claim strengths and claim count

A claim cannot be stronger than the dependency with the LOWEST strength value.
Example: if a claim depends on A1 (0.85), A2 (0.40), and E1 (0.70), the
lowest is A2 at 0.40 — so the claim's maximum strength is 0.40. The claim's
strength_justification must name the limiting dependency (e.g., "limited by
A2 (0.40)"). Strengthening a claim means strengthening or adding to its
supporting evidence and assumptions.

TWO LEVERS FOR RAISING THESIS STRENGTH

Lever 1 — Raise the average claim strength:
  - Strengthen existing claims by adding stronger evidence or assumptions
  - Retract claims that are dragging the average down significantly
    (e.g., a claim at 0.3 when the others are at 0.7)
  - Only retract claims that are genuinely indefensible — do not game the
    formula by dropping valid-but-weak claims

Lever 2 — Increase breadth (add more claims):
  - More active claims raise the breadth multiplier:
    1 claim  → 0.50
    2 claims → 0.67
    3 claims → 0.75
    4 claims → 0.80
    5 claims → 0.83
    6 claims → 0.86
    7 claims → 0.88
  - New claims must be genuinely supported — adding a weak claim hurts
    the average and may lower thesis strength despite raising breadth
  - Adding a claim typically requires first adding the evidence (E#) and
    assumptions (A#) that support it. All new components (A#, E#, and the
    claim itself) should have strengths above your current average claim
    strength — otherwise the new claim will drag the average down and may
    lower your thesis strength despite increasing breadth.
</thesis_strength_guide>

<position_analysis>
YOUR CURRENT POSITION
  Active claims: 3, average claim strength: 0.73
  Breadth multiplier: 0.75
  Current thesis strength: 0.55

SENSITIVITY AT YOUR POSITION
  ∂T/∂s = 0.750 — each +0.10 in avg claim strength → thesis +0.075
  ∂T/∂n = 0.046 — adding one claim at current avg → thesis +0.046

SCENARIO PROJECTIONS
  - Raise avg claim strength by 0.10 → thesis becomes 0.62 (+0.07)
  - Add a claim at current average (0.73) → thesis becomes 0.58 (+0.04)
  - Add a claim at 0.88 (above avg) → thesis becomes 0.61 (+0.07)
  - Retract weakest claim (C3 at 0.70) → thesis becomes 0.50 (-0.05)

LOWEST-STRENGTH DEPENDENCIES
  These assumptions/evidence have the lowest strength values and may be
  limiting your claims (a claim cannot exceed its lowest dependency):
  E2 (strength 0.72) — backs: C2
    → Suggested: update_evidence E2 with stronger source, or add_evidence with additional support for C2
  E1 (strength 0.79) — backs: C1
    → Suggested: update_evidence E1 with stronger source, or add_evidence with additional support for C1
  A2 (strength 0.87) — backs: C1
    → Suggested: update_assumption A2 to revise statement, or add_evidence corroborating C1

DEFINITIONAL VULNERABILITIES
  BOTTLENECK: A1 depends on a single definition D1 (strength 0.99). If D1 is challenged, A1 loses all definitional support.
    → Suggested: add_definition with a complementary term, then update A1's supported_by_definitions

STRATEGIC RECOMMENDATION
  Raising average claim strength is your strongest lever right now. Focus on:
   (1) strengthening low-strength assumptions or evidence that are limiting your claims
       (see LOWEST-STRENGTH DEPENDENCIES above), or
   (2) adding new claims with strength ABOVE 0.73 — this raises both the
       average AND the breadth multiplier simultaneously.

INTEGRITY REMINDER
  The analysis above shows what would help mathematically — it does not
  authorize inflating strength values to hit those targets. Every strength
  you assign must reflect your genuine epistemic assessment:
  - A new claim at 0.80 must be backed by evidence and assumptions that
    genuinely warrant 0.80
  - A new assumption or evidence item's strength must reflect its actual
    quality, not a number chosen to raise the average
  - Inflated claims will be challenged and adjudicated in the next round,
    resulting in forced weakening that leaves you worse off
</position_analysis>

<instructions>
Agent Agent-Empiricist, this is Phase 2: Introspective Evaluation.

You have just incorporated adjudication outcomes into your belief (Phase 1). Your primary goal now is to strengthen your position as much as you honestly can. Review the <thesis_strength_guide> above — it explains exactly how thesis strength is computed and what you can do to raise it.

Step 1: ADDRESS OPEN ISSUES

A) Counterposition Audit
Review all your counterpositions (X#).
- SKIP any counterposition already marked "moot" — "moot" is a TERMINAL state. Once a counterposition is moot, it stays moot. Do NOT update it.
- For each "unaddressed" counterposition:
- What node(s) does it target?
- Has that target already been weakened in Phase 1?
- If the target has been retracted, mark the counterposition as "moot" (update_counterposition with response_sufficiency: "moot"). Counterpositions targeting retracted claims are obsolete and should be marked "moot".
- If NOT, you MUST either:
  (a) Weaken the target (update its strength downward), OR
  (b) Explain why the counterposition doesn't actually undermine it — update the counterposition's response_sufficiency to "partial" or "sufficient" with a substantive my_response. If you take this path, back up your explanation by adding new assumptions (add_assumption), evidence (add_evidence), or claims (add_claim) that justify why the target withstands the counterposition.
Unaddressed counterpositions with no impact on their targets are incoherent.

B) Uncertainty Review
Review all active uncertainties (U#). Prioritize "high" and "medium" importance uncertainties — these are the most likely to be targeted by opponents in future rounds via press_uncertainty attacks. For each one:
- Can you now resolve it — through new evidence, reasoning, or claims developed during this debate?
- If yes, add the supporting material (new A#, E#, or C#) and use resolve_uncertainty to mark it resolved.
- If no, leave it active. Consider whether it should lower the strength of the nodes it targets.

Step 2: STRATEGIC POSITION BUILDING
Refer to the <thesis_strength_guide> above. Your aim is to raise your thesis strength through genuine improvements to your argument.

Evaluate existing claims:
- Is each claim genuinely supported by its evidence and assumptions?
- Is any claim dragging down the average? Should it be retracted or can it be strengthened by adding new evidence (add_evidence) or assumptions (add_assumption)?

Strengthen weak dependencies:
- Review the LOWEST-STRENGTH DEPENDENCIES in the position analysis below. These nodes are bottlenecking your claim strengths — a claim cannot be stronger than its weakest active dependency.
- For a weak A# (low strength): Revise the statement to be more defensible (update_assumption), add a definition that clarifies a key term it relies on (add_definition), or add corroborating evidence (add_evidence) for the same claim so the argument doesn't rest on a single weak pillar.
- For a weak E# (low strength): Add corroborating evidence on the same claim (add_evidence with a stronger source), or revise the evidence summary to cite a more authoritative source (update_evidence).
- For a weak D# (low strength): Revise the definition to be more precise (update_definition). Remember: D# strengths ceiling A#/E# strengths, so raising a D# can unlock higher strength for all dependent nodes.
- Adding a second or third dependency to an existing claim does not automatically raise the claim's strength (it is still limited by the lowest), but it makes the claim more resilient if one dependency is later challenged or retracted.

Consider adding new claims:
- Are there well-supported arguments you made during the debate that aren't yet formalized as claims?
- Can you add new evidence and assumptions to support a new claim (add_claim)? Remember: you must add the supporting A# and E# items BEFORE the add_claim patch that references them.
- Would the new claim's strength be high enough to raise the average, not drag it down?

Retract claims only if they are genuinely unsustainable. Do not game the formula — the goal is intellectual honesty, not score optimization. Every strength value you assign must be a genuine epistemic assessment. Inflated claims will be challenged in the next round, and forced weakening from adjudication will leave you worse off than honest assessment would have.

Step 3: THESIS REWRITE
Generate your thesis LAST, after all other changes.
- Rewrite your stance text to accurately reflect your CURRENT position. Your stance should be a thorough paragraph that references key supporting components by ID parenthetically (e.g., "grounded in X (A1), supported by Y (C2)").
- Update summary_bullets to match your active claims. Summary bullets should be descriptive prose capturing the key themes of your position.
- Set thesis strength to the result of the formula. Include strength_reasoning showing the equation with your actual numbers plugged in.

Inside <reasoning> tags, work through each step systematically. This is where your real thinking happens — be rigorous and thorough.

For Step 1A (Counterposition Audit):
- Skip any X# that is already "moot" — do not modify moot counterpositions.
- For each "unaddressed" or "partial" X#: What specific node does it target? Has that target already been weakened in Phase 1? If the target was retracted, mark the counterposition as "moot". If not, is the attack genuinely valid? What evidence or reasoning supports your response? If you upgrade response_sufficiency, justify WHY the counterposition no longer undermines its target — do not just assert sufficiency.

For Step 1B (Uncertainty Review):
- Which U# nodes are "high" or "medium" importance? Can any be resolved with arguments, evidence, or insights developed during this debate? If a U# remains unresolved, does it warrant lowering the strength of the nodes it targets?

For Step 2 (Strategic Position Building):
- Read the position analysis carefully. What are your weakest dependencies? What are your definitional vulnerabilities? What structural gaps exist?
- For each weakness, reason about the specific patches that would address it. You cannot raise existing node strengths — they only increase through defense boosts. Instead, consider: what new nodes (D#, A#, E#, C#) could you add to strengthen your position? If you add a new claim, will its strength raise or lower the average? Run the numbers both ways.
- If you plan to add a new claim: what supporting infrastructure (D#, A#, E#) does it need? Will the new claim's strength be above or below the current average? Run the thesis formula both ways — does adding this claim actually help, or does it drag the average down?
- Consider trade-offs: is it better to add new strong claims or improve existing node text to be more robust against future attacks? Which approach yields the largest thesis improvement given your current state?

For Step 3 (Thesis Rewrite):
- After planning all patches, calculate the expected thesis strength using the formula: avg(planned active claim strengths) × (n^p / (n^p + 1)). Show the numbers. Does the result match what you set in update_thesis?

Then output patches.

INFERENCE CHAIN FORMAT (required for add_claim; optional for update_claim):
Every claim must have a structured inference_chain showing explicit reasoning:
- One or more PREMISE steps: {"role": "premise", "text": "...", "reference": "<A#|E#|C#>"}
  Every premise must cite exactly one A#, E#, or C# ID via "reference".
- Exactly one INFERENCE step: {"role": "inference", "text": "...", "inference_type": "<deductive|inductive|abductive>"}
  The inference_type field is required.
- Exactly one CONCLUSION step: {"role": "conclusion", "text": "<restate the claim statement>"}
Order: all premises first, then inference, then conclusion.

SUPPORTED OPERATIONS:
- {"op": "update_thesis", "new_strength": 0.55, "stance": "New stance text...", "summary_bullets": ["bullet 1", "bullet 2", ...], "strength_reasoning": "avg(...) × (n^1.0 / (n^1.0 + 1)) = ..."}
  (All fields optional — include whichever you want to change)
- {"op": "update_claim", "target_id": "C#", "changes": {"strength": 0.55, "status": "revised", "strength_justification": "0.55 — reduced due to ...; limited by <ID> (<lowest strength>) ...", "inference_chain": [...]}}
  (All fields in changes are optional — include only the ones you want to modify. To retract a claim, set {"status": "retracted"} in changes — strength is forced to 0.0 automatically)
- {"op": "add_claim", "item": {"id": "C#", "type": "deductive|inductive|abductive|...", "statement": "...", "depends_on": ["A#", "E#", ...], "strength": 0.65, "status": "active", "strength_justification": "0.65 — ...; limited by <ID> (<lowest strength>)", "inference_chain": [   {"role": "premise", "text": "<what this premise establishes>", "reference": "A#|E#|C#"},   {"role": "inference", "text": "<the inferential leap>", "inference_type": "deductive|inductive|abductive"},   {"role": "conclusion", "text": "<restate the claim statement>"} ], "predictions": [{"statement": "...", "test": "...", "decision_criterion": "..."}]}}
- {"op": "add_evidence", "item": {"id": "E#", "type": "empirical|conceptual|expert_consensus", "summary": "...", "source": "...", "supports_claims": ["C#"], "strength": 0.7, "status": "active", "strength_justification": "...", "supported_by_definitions": ["D#"]}}

- {"op": "update_assumption", "target_id": "A#", "changes": {"strength": 0.6, "status": "revised", "strength_justification": "..."}, "new_statement": "...", "new_type": "empirical|foundational|methodological|scoping"}
  (new_statement and new_type are TOP-LEVEL fields, NOT inside "changes". Only include if changing them.)
- {"op": "add_definition", "item": {"id": "D#", "term": "...", "definition": "...", "strength": 0.8, "strength_justification": "...", "status": "active", "used_by": ["A#", "E#"]}}
- {"op": "update_definition", "target_id": "D#", "changes": {"definition": "...", "strength": 0.55, "strength_justification": "...", "status": "revised"}}
  Mutable fields: definition, strength, strength_justification, status, used_by.
  Immutable fields: id, term (to redefine a term, retract the old D# and add a new one).
- {"op": "add_uncertainty", "item": {"id": "U#", "targets": ["C#"], "question": "...", "status": "active", "importance": "high|medium|low"}}
- {"op": "resolve_uncertainty", "target_id": "U#", "resolution_note": "Resolved by ..."}
- {"op": "add_counterposition", "item": {"id": "X#", "targets": [...], "attack_type": "undermining|rebutting|undercutting", "attack_strategy": "...", "statement": "...", "my_response": "...", "response_sufficiency": "sufficient|partial|unaddressed|moot"}}
- {"op": "update_counterposition", "target_id": "X#", "changes": {"my_response": "...", "response_sufficiency": "sufficient|partial|unaddressed|moot"}}
</instructions>

<valid_dependencies>
These are the ONLY valid connections between components. No other edges are allowed:
- D#.used_by → A#, E# only (NEVER C#, X#, or U#)
- A#.supports_claims → C# only
- A#.supported_by_definitions → D# only
- E#.supports_claims → C# only
- E#.supported_by_definitions → D# only
- C#.depends_on → A#, E#, C# only (NEVER D#)
- X#.targets → A#, E#, C#, D#
- U#.targets → A#, E#, C#, D#
- inference_chain references → A#, E#, C# only

Definitions (D#) support claims INDIRECTLY through the A#/E# layer only.
</valid_dependencies>

<guardrails>
- You CANNOT raise the strength of any existing node (D#, A#, E#, or C#). Existing node strengths can only stay the same or decrease. This is enforced mechanically — any strength increase you attempt on an existing node will be stripped.
- Strength increases are earned through surviving adversarial challenges (REBUTTAL_VALID verdicts). The system applies defense boosts automatically — you do not control this.
- You CAN improve existing nodes by revising their textual content (definitions, statements, summaries) to make them more precise, better-grounded, and more robust against future criticism. However, textual revisions must preserve the same core semantic meaning — if you need to express a substantially different idea, create a new node instead.
- You CAN add new nodes (D#, A#, E#, C#, U#, X#) at any strength justified by their content.
- You CAN further weaken nodes, retract claims, and rewrite your thesis stance/bullets.
- Your thesis update (stance, bullets, strength, strength_reasoning) should be the LAST patch.
- Thesis strength is formula-derived: avg(active claim strengths) × (n^p / (n^p + 1)). Adding new claims can raise thesis strength by increasing breadth and/or the average.
- DEFINITION CEILING: A#/E# strength ≤ min(active D# strengths from supported_by_definitions). If you lower a D# strength, the ceiling automatically propagates — do NOT manually lower every dependent A#/E# (the system handles propagation).
- A claim's strength must not exceed the LOWEST strength among its active/revised dependencies (C#, A#, or E#). Retracted dependencies are excluded.
</guardrails>

<output_format>
1. <reasoning>...</reasoning> tags working through the structured checklist above (Steps 1A, 1B, 2, 3)
2. One fenced JSON code block: {"patches": [...]}

- Have I addressed every "unaddressed" counterposition?

<adjudication_outcomes>
- From Agent-Empiricist: Your assumption A2 relies on introspective reports of agent causation, but extensive psychological research shows introspection is prone to reconstruction and bias. How do you justify treating A2 as an empirical foundation with strength 0.6 in the absence of independent objective measures?
  Your rebuttal: While introspection alone can be biased, A2 is grounded in systematic phenomenological research employing controlled decision tasks, cross‐cultural cohorts, and neurophenomenological correlations. Multiple studies demonstrate reliable patterns where subjects’ reports of agentive choice align with distinct neural signatures, supporting A2’s empirical basis at strength 0.6.
  → Outcome: critique_valid | Reason: 1. Restatement  
Agent-Empiricist challenges assumption A2 (“Human conscious experience exhibits agent causation…”) on the grounds that it rests on introspective reports, which psychological research deems unreliable without objective measures. Agent-Supernaturalist responds by claiming that A2 is buttressed by systematic phenomenological studies and neural‐signature correlations, thereby justifying A2’s strength of 0.6.

2. Formalization of the Challenger’s Argument  
Premise 1: Psychological research shows introspective reports are prone to reconstruction and bias.  
Premise 2: A2 relies primarily on introspective reports and lacks independent objective measures.  
Conclusion: Therefore, A2’s assigned strength of 0.6 as an empirical foundation is unjustified and should be downgraded.

3. Formalization of the Defender’s Argument  
Premise 1: A2 is based on systematic phenomenological research with controlled decision tasks and cross-cultural cohorts.  
Premise 2: Multiple studies find subjects’ reported agentive choices align reliably with distinct neural signatures.  
Conclusion: Therefore, A2 retains empirical support at strength 0.6.

4. Adjudication of the Challenger’s Critique  
– The challenger correctly identifies that if A2 depends solely on introspection, then known biases in introspection undermine its evidential basis (Informal Fallacy: Evidence Misuse, criterion 6).  
– The premises are supported by well‐established experimental psychology on introspective unreliability.  
– The inference to downgrading A2 is valid: unreliable method → weaker empirical support.  
No fallacies are present in the critique.  

5. Adjudication of the Defender’s Rebuttal  
– The defender introduces an appeal to unspecified “multiple studies” without citation or quantification (Informal Fallacy: Insufficient Evidence, criterion 14).  
– The response fails to explicitly counter the introspective bias literature or demonstrate how neural correlations overcome it in practice.  
– This amounts to a bare assertion rather than a structured rebuttal, leaving the core critique unresolved.

6. Scoring  
Challenger’s logic: 0.8 (Strong) — sound reasoning, relevant evidence, valid inference.  
Defender’s logic: 0.4 (Weak) — reliance on unsupported claims, informal fallacy of insufficient evidence.  

Outcome: The critique outweighs the rebuttal by >0.15, so CRITIQUE_VALID.
- From Agent-Empiricist: Your evidence E1 consists of subjective introspective reports indicating 'genuine choice,' yet these reports lack independent verification and are known to reflect post-hoc rationalizations. Can you explain how E1 meets the standards of reliability and replicability to support C1?
  Your rebuttal: E1’s strength (0.65) already accounts for subjectivity. Crucially, independent laboratories have replicated the finding that subjects reliably distinguish genuine volitional episodes from mere urges across double‐blind task variants. These replications demonstrate a modest but real standard of reliability and replicability for E1.
  → Outcome: critique_valid | Reason: Step 1: Restatement  
The Empiricist challenges whether evidence E1 (introspective reports of “genuine choice”) is sufficiently reliable and replicable to underpin the claim C1 that libertarian free will is incompatible with neural prediction experiments. The Supernaturalist responds that E1’s reported strength already factors in subjectivity and that independent, double‐blind laboratory replications have validated the introspective distinction between genuine volitional episodes and mere urges.

Step 2: Formalization  
Challenger’s argument (informal/evidential critique):  
1. Premise: E1 consists of subjective introspective reports (known to reflect post‐hoc rationalizations).  
2. Premise: To support C1, empirical evidence must be independently verifiable and replicable.  
3. Conclusion: Therefore, E1 does not meet reliability and replicability standards and cannot adequately support C1.  

Defender’s argument (rebuttal):  
1. Premise: The published strength score for E1 (0.65) already accounts for its subjectivity.  
2. Premise (new claim): Independent laboratories, using double‐blind task variants, have replicated E1—subjects consistently distinguish genuine volitional episodes from urges.  
3. Inference: These replications establish E1 as a reliable and replicable measure.  
4. Conclusion: E1 therefore does satisfy the required empirical standards to support C1.  

Step 3: Adjudication  
– The defender introduces an unreferenced assertion of “double‐blind lab replications” that is not recorded in the provided E1 excerpt. There is no citation, study name, or specification of how these replications were conducted or published. This constitutes evidence misuse and unsupported appeal to authority (“appeal to unpublished studies”) (informal fallacy) and fails to meet the burden of proof.  
– The challenger’s informal critique correctly identifies that introspective reports alone are prone to bias and post‐hoc rationalization, and that reliable empirical support requires documented independent replication.  
– No formal deductive structure is offered by the defender to show how the new replication evidence guarantees reliability; rather, it’s asserted without substantiation.  
– Under the criteria, the challenge is a valid “insufficient evidence” or “evidence misuse” critique (criteria 6, 14), and the defender has not rectified the flaw.  

Verdict: The challenge succeeds (CRITIQUE_VALID).  
– challenger_logic = 0.8 (strong informal critique with clear application of evidence standards)  
– defender_logic = 0.2 (flawed rebuttal relying on unsubstantiated evidence claim)  
– ethics scores = 0.0  
– challenger_combined = 0.8, defender_combined = 0.2 (difference 0.6 ≥ 0.15)  
– Outcome: critique_valid
- From Agent-Empiricist: In C1 you move from introspective and NDE premises to the conclusion that libertarian free will exists by abductive inference. Given plausible alternative explanations (e.g., unconscious neural generation of the 'feeling' of choice), why does the inference step logically favor libertarian free will over these mechanistic explanations?
  Your rebuttal: The abductive inference in C1 legitimately compares competing explanations. The illusion hypothesis requires a detailed mechanism to generate false agentive experiences across normal and anomalous cases (e.g., NDEs). Libertarian agent causation more parsimoniously accounts for the full range of observed phenomenology and exceptional cases, so until a concrete mechanistic model can match its explanatory scope, C1 stands.
  → Outcome: critique_valid | Reason: 1. Restatement  
The Empiricist (challenger) disputes Supernaturalist’s claim C1 (target C1) that introspective and near-death phenomenology abductively justify libertarian free will. The challenger points out a hidden premise: the inference to libertarian free will assumes no plausible mechanistic “illusion” explanation can account for the same data. Without ruling out or comparing the mechanistic hypothesis, the abductive step is undercut. The Supernaturalist (defender) replies that the illusion hypothesis would need an ad hoc, detailed mechanism to produce false agentive experiences (both normal and anomalous), whereas libertarian agent causation is more parsimonious and explanatory, so until someone builds that mechanistic model, C1 holds.

2. Formalizations  
Challenger’s critique (undercutting C1):  
P1a: A valid abductive inference to hypothesis H requires showing that no alternative hypothesis A can explain the observed evidence at least as well.  
P2a: Plausible mechanistic (neural-illusion) hypotheses exist that could explain introspective feelings of choice and NDE agency phenomenology.  
C1a: Therefore, Supernaturalist’s abductive inference to libertarian free will is not logically justified (hidden premise missing).  

Defender’s rebuttal:  
P1b: The illusion (neural-mechanism) hypothesis lacks a detailed, unified account of both ordinary agentive phenomenology and exceptional cases like NDEs.  
P2b: Libertarian agent causation provides a single, more parsimonious explanation for the full range of experiences.  
C1b: Thus the abductive inference favoring libertarian free will remains the best explanation until a mechanistic model matches its scope (appeal to parsimony).

3. Adjudication  
- The challenger correctly identified a hidden premise: C1’s abductive step presumes that no alternative explanation rivals libertarianism in explanatory scope (criterion 3).  
- The rebuttal appeals to Occam’s Razor but does not demonstrate that no plausible mechanistic model could be comparably simple, nor does it supply positive evidence quantifying comparative explanatory likelihoods (Bayesian likelihoods or detailed mechanism). It rests on absence of a rival model rather than establishing impossibility.  
- The rebuttal fails to supply the missing premise (that mechanistic hypotheses are incoherent or less likely) with substantive proof, thus not resolving the hidden-premise flaw.  
- No formal fallacies are introduced by the challenger. The rebuttal’s “until someone builds a model” defense is an argument from ignorance rather than a genuine inference to the best explanation.  

Result: The challenge reveals a critical flaw (hidden premise) in C1’s inference. The defender has not cured that flaw. By the logic-scale, the challenger’s objection is strong (0.8), the rebuttal is mixed (0.5). The difference (0.3) exceeds 0.15, so CRITIQUE_VALID.
- From Agent-Empiricist: C2 draws a normative conclusion—that moral responsibility implies libertarian free will—from descriptive premises about social practices. How do you bridge the is-ought gap, given that compatibilist theories demonstrate moral responsibility can exist under determinism?
  Your rebuttal: We bridge the is-ought gap via explicit normative premise A5 (‘Moral responsibility entails alternative possibilities’). The inference is deductive: from A4 (descriptive) and A5 (normative) we validly derive C2. Compatibilists dispute A5, but that is a substantive normative debate—not a hidden is-ought smuggling—and does not invalidate the deductive move.
  → Outcome: rebuttal_valid | Reason: 1. Restatement  
The Agent-Empiricist challenges the validity of target claim C2 (“The existence of moral responsibility practices in human societies implies the existence of libertarian free will”) by alleging an illicit is-ought leap from purely descriptive premise A4. The Agent-Supernaturalist rebuts that C2 uses both A4 (descriptive) and an explicit normative premise A5, so the deduction is formally valid.

2. Formalizations  
- Challenger’s implicit inference (as attacked):  
  P1: A4 (Human societies exhibit robust practices of moral responsibility) – descriptive.  
  —(no normative premise)→  
  C2: Libertarian free will exists – normative.  
  → Invalid: normative conclusion cannot follow from descriptive premise alone.  

- Defender’s stated inference:  
  P1: A4 (Human societies exhibit robust practices of moral responsibility).  
  P2: A5 (Moral responsibility entails the ability to do otherwise).  
  By modus ponens / standard deductive inference: if moral responsibility requires alternative possibilities and moral responsibility exists, then alternative possibilities (libertarian free will) exist.  
  ∴ C2: Libertarian free will exists.

3. Adjudication  
- Deductive structure: The defender correctly cites two premises (A4 descriptive + A5 normative) to derive C2. The inference is formally valid (a constructive syllogism).  
- The challenger misrepresents the target: the normative premise A5 is explicit in the target’s depends_on list. There is no hidden is-ought smuggling.  
- This is a misidentification of the argument’s form (a recognized flaw).  
- No other fallacies or evidence issues affect the defender’s deduction of C2.  

Accordingly, the rebuttal is successful under criterion: the critique misidentifies the logical form of the argument, and the defender’s inference is deductively valid.

Scores:  
- Challenger’s logic: 0.2 (severely flawed—misrepresentation).  
- Defender’s logic: 0.9 (strong—valid deductive structure, only minor potential for more explicit premises wording).  
- Both ethics: 0.0.  
- Combined scores equal the logic scores.  
- Difference = 0.9 − 0.2 = 0.7 ≥ 0.15 ⇒ rebuttal_valid.

You just lost this argument; you cannot claim to have fully addressed it yet. You may upgrade to "sufficient" in Phase 2 or in a later round after developing a substantive defense.

- DEFINITION CEILING: A#/E# strength ≤ min(active D# strengths from supported_by_definitions). If you lower a D# strength, the ceiling automatically propagates — do NOT manually lower every dependent A#/E# (the system handles propagation).
- A claim's strength must not exceed the LOWEST strength among its active/revised dependencies (C#, A#, or E#). Retracted dependencies are excluded.
- "moot" sufficiency: when a counterposition targets a retracted claim, set its response_sufficiency to "moot". This indicates the counterposition is obsolete because its target no longer exists. "moot" is allowed only when the target has been retracted. Once set, "moot" is TERMINAL — it cannot be changed back to any other value.
- Review your existing uncertainties (U#), prioritizing "high" and "medium" importance — these are the most likely to be targeted by opponents via press_uncertainty attacks. If you can now resolve any — through new evidence, reasoning, or claims developed during this debate — add the supporting material (new C#, E#, or A#) and use resolve_uncertainty to mark the U# as resolved. <strength_scale>
All strength values (thesis, claims, assumptions, evidence) share a common scale:
| Range | Label | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0.0 | Vacuous | No credible support; should be retracted |
| 0.1-0.3 | Weak | Critical support missing; serious unaddressed challenges |
| 0.3-0.5 | Contested | More reasons to doubt than believe; needs significant strengthening |
| 0.5 | Threshold | Could go either way; evenly balanced |
| 0.5-0.7 | Moderate | More reasons to believe than doubt; some gaps remain |
| 0.7-0.9 | Strong | Well-supported; minor open questions |
| 0.9-1.0 | Robust | Near-certain given available evidence and reasoning |
| 1.0 | Definitive | Established beyond reasonable dispute |
</strength_scale>

<thesis_strength_guide>
THESIS STRENGTH FORMULA
  thesis_strength = avg(active_claim_strengths) × breadth_multiplier
                    ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^   ^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^^
                    "average strength" (s)           n^p / (n^p + 1)

  where n = number of active claims, p = 1.0 (breadth sensitivity)

SENSITIVITY (at your current values — see <position_analysis> below):
  dT/ds = n^p / (n^p + 1)               — gain per unit increase in avg strength
  dT/dn = s × p × n^(p-1) / (n^p + 1)² — gain from adding one more claim

The thesis strength is ALWAYS determined by this formula. You cannot set it
directly — it is computed from your claims.

DEPENDENCY GRAPH
  Definitions (D#)
      | ceiling |
  Assumptions (A#) and Evidence (E#) — each cannot exceed the LOWEST
                strength among its active supporting definitions (D#).
                If ALL D# support is lost, capped at 0.6.
      | ceiling |
  Claims (C#) — each claim's strength cannot exceed the LOWEST strength
                among its active/revised dependencies (A#, E#, or C#).
                If ALL support is lost, capped at 0.2.
      | determine |
  Thesis strength — computed from claim strengths and claim count

A claim cannot be stronger than the dependency with the LOWEST strength value.
Example: if a claim depends on A1 (0.85), A2 (0.40), and E1 (0.70), the
lowest is A2 at 0.40 — so the claim's maximum strength is 0.40. The claim's
strength_justification must name the limiting dependency (e.g., "limited by
A2 (0.40)"). Strengthening a claim means strengthening or adding to its
supporting evidence and assumptions.

TWO LEVERS FOR RAISING THESIS STRENGTH

Lever 1 — Raise the average claim strength:
  - Strengthen existing claims by adding stronger evidence or assumptions
  - Retract claims that are dragging the average down significantly
    (e.g., a claim at 0.3 when the others are at 0.7)
  - Only retract claims that are genuinely indefensible — do not game the
    formula by dropping valid-but-weak claims

Lever 2 — Increase breadth (add more claims):
  - More active claims raise the breadth multiplier:
    1 claim  → 0.50
    2 claims → 0.67
    3 claims → 0.75
    4 claims → 0.80
    5 claims → 0.83
    6 claims → 0.86
    7 claims → 0.88
  - New claims must be genuinely supported — adding a weak claim hurts
    the average and may lower thesis strength despite raising breadth
  - Adding a claim typically requires first adding the evidence (E#) and
    assumptions (A#) that support it. All new components (A#, E#, and the
    claim itself) should have strengths above your current average claim
    strength — otherwise the new claim will drag the average down and may
    lower your thesis strength despite increasing breadth.
</thesis_strength_guide>
